Preface
This master thesis is a result of a 30 ECTS point thesis performed at the Techni-
cal University of Denmark in the time frame of the 23rd August 2021 to the 23rd
January 2022 and concludes a two and a half year Master of Science degree in Civil
Engineering, MSc Eng (Building Technology) at the Technical University of Den-
mark. The project is supervised by Phillip Skov Halding, Assistant Professor at the
Department of Civil Engineering, DTU.

==PAGE:4==
Abstract
The use of arches in modern buildings is a greatly underutilized construction method
when designing main structural components.
This is largely due to the greater
costs typically associated with the construction as addition specialised framework is
needed. The Pearl-chain technology, invented by Kristian Dahl Hertz at the Techno-
logical University of Denmark [1], presents a solution to the increased costs of arched
structures. The technology allows for multiple concrete elements of identical size to
be post-tensioned together creating an arched shape. This thesis investigates the
applicability of pearl-chain arches in stable type buildings. The applicability of the
arch is determined from the static veriп¬Ғcation, environmental impact and total costs
compared to currently constructed stables with primary structural systems of either
wood or steel.
The static veriп¬Ғcation of bearing capacity for each pearl-chain arch is based on a
theoretical examination of arch types and their static behaviour when modifying
span, rise and arch center line curve. The examination explains the change in static
behaviour when supports conditions are changed and which type of arch at which
span and rise is to prefer when designing a stable. The preferred design combines
a minimal occurrence of bending moments and axial load with a span and rise gov-
erned by dimensions similar to the reference stables. To design the most optimal
arch and avoid previously know errors in construction a study of modern arches is
made evaluating diп¬Җerent arched structures and their static functions. This includes
arches of steel, wood and concrete and the solution to dealing with a large span and
the addition of horizontal thrust in all material cases is described.
Finally the actions on the arch are calculated in accordance with the corresponding
Eurocode and combined in load combinations to showcase critical load cases. Each
load combinations is calculated using the FEM program Autodesk Robot and the
resulting sectional forces and reactions are used as the basis for structural veriп¬Ғca-
tion in the ultimate- and serviceability limit state. The structural components are
veriп¬Ғed in accordance with EN-1992-1 and consists of the concrete and tension steel
reinforcement in the arch and well as the foundations.
The environmental impact of each arch is quantiп¬Ғed by a life cycle assessment (LCA)
which states the global warming potential (GWP) in equivalent CO2 emission for
each material from the production-, usage- and end-of-life stages. The assessment
shows a 55 % decrease in total GWP when compared to the steel stable types but
as 127 % increase when compared to the wooden stable types.
iii
==PAGE:5==
Lastly the costs of the pearl-chain arches are estimated by use of material retail
prices and compared to the other stable types. The comparison shows a decrease in
price in both cases with savings of 35.1 % compared to the wooden stable types and
68.4 % compared to the steel stable types.
iv

Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Background
The primary structural parts of modern stable buildings has remained relatively
unchanged for decades, with the main structural components in each frame being
of either wood or steel. The implementation of concrete in these structure types as
been largely unseen due to the great cost of production associated. The invention of
the pearl-chain technology, by professor Christian Hertz at the Technical University
of Denmark, introduces a method of construction which greatly reduces the costs of
construction [1]. This thesis investigates whether itвҖҷs theoretically possible to replace
the current structural stable frames with pearl-chain technology arches and how the
solution compares to the current frames in both cost and environmental impact.
The arch solution will be implemented in two real-life pig-stable cases provided by
SEGES which includes criteria for minimum usable volume and a showcase of the
amount of diп¬Җerent material component used in the pig-stable. The two stables pro-
vided are one with a primary structural system of steel and one of wood.
As the pearl-chain concrete solution is п¬Ғnalised the environmental impact and costs
of materials will be calculated and compared to the steel- and wooden stable.
1.2
Scope
This thesis focuses on the potential reduction in construction costs and CO2 emission
by replacing the traditional bearing system in the roof, walls and foundation for two
SEGES stable buildings.
The fulп¬Ғlment of this scope results in:
1

Chapter 1
Section 1.3
вҖў A analysis and simulation of critical load combinations on the pearl-chain struc-
ture.
вҖў Static analysis and veriп¬Ғcation of suggested concrete arch solution.
вҖў Veriп¬Ғcation of the foundation and underlying load-bearing soil.
вҖў Global Warming Potential (GWP) of the suggested solution compared to the
GWP of the other stable types.
вҖў Cost estimate of the suggested solution compared to the estimated costs of the
other stable types.
1.3
Readers guide
This thesis is divided into main chapters each covering a major part of either un-
derstanding of the theory or explanations of the proposed solutions. Each chapters
content is elaborated below to give the reader a quick overview.
вҖў Chapter 2 - Arches.
A theoretical examination of the arches. A showcase of history, terminology,
geometrical design criteria, static behaviour and modern arch implementation.
вҖў Chapter 3 - Pearl-chain Reinforcement.
A presentation and quick overview of the theoretical and practical aspects
used in the pearl-chain reinforcement principle including the showcase of a
constructed previous implementation of the principle.
вҖў Chapter 4 - Load Actions.
A elaboration of load types and their behaviour on arched roof structures.
вҖў Chapter 5 - Proposed arch design.
The design process of the proposed arch design is elaborated upon which in-
cludes the design criteria and construction process for the arches as well as the
veriп¬Ғcation of bearing capacity in each load combination.
вҖў Chapter 6 - Life Cycle Analysis.
A calculation and comparison of the GWP for pearl-chain arch and the two
other stable types.
вҖў Chapter 7 - Material Cost Estimation
A calculation and comparison of the total material costs for the pearl-chain
arch and the two other stable types.

Chapter 2
Arches
2.1
History
The principle of arched shape structures dates back to pre-historic times and orig-
inates from the natural occurrence of arched shapes. Noticeable natural arches are
located in the southern part of the USA where rock formations had formed an open-
ing underneath. The openings occurred due to centuries of erosion which over time
removes part of the rock. Seen in п¬Ғgure 2.1 is the natural arch in Arches National
Park.
Figure 2.1: The Delicate Arch in Arches National Park, Utah, United States [2]
3800BC is the earliest known use of the arched shape where the Mesopotamian people
developed arched structural bearing systems for entrances into caves [3]. The arch
is a true-keystone arch which describes the п¬Ғnal stone at the highest point as the
keystone due the arch having no structural capabilities until this key part is placed.
The п¬Ғrst arch was discovered during an underground excavation of the Sumerian city
Nippur. The Nippur arch is illustrated in п¬Ғgure 2.2.
4
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2
Section 2.1
Figure 2.2: Mesopotamian Nippur Arch [4]
The arched shapes ability to withstand vertical loads proved to be the best struc-
tural system as the time and the Sumerian people found it to be the best solution
for building an opening of any sort.
The limitations of material and craftsmanship at the time favored the solution as only
pure man power was needed for the construction process. The solutions remained
simple and small until the roman empire systematically implemented the arch design
in most of their structures. Noticeable in their solutions were the spans at which the
arches were implemented as the span now reached multiple meters in their aqueduct
constructions as seen in п¬Ғgure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Roman aqueduct [5]
The arch remained a widely used structural solution as the shape utilized the com-
pression strength of the stone blocks used and solved a common structural problem;
5
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 2
Section 2.2
tensile stresses. Come 1849 the same problem received a new solution. The prin-
ciple of utilizing materials to handle tension stresses in tension weak materials was
invented by Joseph Monier [6] which shortly after yielded the reinforced concrete
solution. The solution of reinforcing concrete with steel bars caused the structural
arches to be outdated as the straight concrete elements were easier and faster to both
produce and construct. This has caused arches in modern constructions to be purely
aesthetic.
The recently invented pearl-chain reinforcement makes use of the structural beneп¬Ғts
associated with the arched shape while incorporating the strength of reinforced con-
crete all in one solution. The pearl-chain reinforcement solution could prove a valid
future solution [7]. This remains to be seen.
2.2
Shapes & Types
The shape of an arch is deп¬Ғned as a symmetrical element spanning between two
points and creating an opening as a result [8]. The terminology to describe diп¬Җerent
central parameters throughout the arch is shown in п¬Ғgure 2.4 [9].
Span
Rise
Springings
Crown
Directix
Intrados
Extrados
Figure 2.4: Arch Terminology
вҖў Crown: Highest point of the arch
вҖў Span (L): The horizontal distance between the supports
вҖў Rise (f): The vertical distance from the zero line to the crown
вҖў Springings: Point at which the arch вҖҷspringsвҖҷ from the supports
6
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2
Section 2.2
вҖў Directix: Center line of the arch
вҖў Extrados: Outer part of the arch
вҖў Intrados: Inner part of the arch
A deп¬Ғning characteristic of arches, compared to the usual straight structural element,
is the presence of a horizontal thrust occurring at the springings when subjected
to load [3]. The thrust therefore demands additional horizontal resistance at each
support for the arch to maintain itвҖҷs shape.
Traditionally when using arches in structures the function is limited to an arched
shape material (bricks, mortar, steel, wood or concrete) between two supports. Such
arches used in structures can be divided into four main static types; Three-hinged,
two-hinged, п¬Ғxed and one-hinged [9]. The four types are shown in п¬Ғgure 2.5.
Fixed (Hingeless)
Fixed (One-hinged)
Simple (Three-hinged)
Simple (Two-hinged)
Figure 2.5: Static Arch Types
The п¬Ғxed arches are characterised by the support conditions at the springings be-
ing completely rigid and therefore being restrained against vertical and horizontal
displacements as well as rotations. The simple arches, both two- and three-hinged,
have support conditions at the springings restraining displacement in the vertical
and horizontal direction.
The four arch types can be further sub-divided into two static categories; statically
determinate and statically in-determinate. Statically in-determinate structures are
structures that cannot be analyzed using statics, or equations of equilibrium only, as
the number of unknown reactions are greater than the amount of static equilibrium
equations. This causes each potential estimation of reactions by hand to be a function
of the other reactions and therefore not a valid solution.
The static equilibrium

the equilibrium of vertical- and horizontal forces as well as the moment.

The degree of static in-determinancy is then established as the number of reactions
greater than the amount of equilibrium equations. As a result only the three-hinged
arch can be considered statically determinate. This present the п¬Ғrst major decision
when designing an arched building as the calculations of statically determined struc-
tures are signiп¬Ғcantly easier than those of the statically in-determined structures
and if no simulation programs are available the statically determinate arch is to be
preferred. As the three-hinged arch functions with a hinge at the crown the resulting
stiп¬Җness of the entire arch is lower than that of the other arch types. This has to be
kept in mind if the arch is placed in poor soil conditions as itвҖҷll be more likely to de-
formation if insigniп¬Ғcant horizontal bearing capacity is present in the foundation [9].
The considerations in choosing the optimal arch are further elaborated in chapter 2.5.

Catenary curve
Last in the typical arch center line curves is the catenary arch. The catenary arch
is an arch type which is dependent on the weight distribution of the arch structure
itself [10]. The arch is created from an inverted catenary curve which is the naturally
occurring arch shape in hanging chains. The chain is supported at each end and, by
itвҖҷs own self-weight, forms the catenary curve as a function of the gravitational pull
on the chain material as seen in п¬Ғgure 2.6.
Figure 2.6: Catenary curve and inverted curve [11]
This method of creating an arch was one of the п¬Ғrst principles of creating a strong
arch as the shape represents a zero moment structure when the structure is only

The method of analysis for the occurring section forces throughout the arch is pre-
sented in this chapter. Of the four arch types shown in п¬Ғgure 2.5 the three- and two
hinged arches are showcased as these represent the two arch types possibly used later
in this project.

Three hinged

as a result. For this elaboration of the static behaviour of the three-hinged arch itвҖҷs

==PAGE:26==

each load acting on the arch can be directly translated to the reference beam as the
size of the force and distance from their respective support remains unchanged. It
can therefore be assumed that the vertical reactions for the arch and reference beam
are equal in size and direction and that the vertical reactions are found similarly by
moment- and vertical equilibrium.
Continuing to use the reference beam the moment at point C in the reference beam
yields a diп¬Җerence as the arch structure introduces a hinge at the crown. Resulting
from this the moment at the crown is zero and the correlation described in equation
2.10 becomes apparent as the contribution from the horizontal thrust is taken into
account which yields an easy solution to determine the horizontal reactions.

Equation 2.10 is only valid if the arch is subjected to vertical loads only. By the
introduction of horizontal forces, such as wind pressure and suction, the horizontal
reactions at A and B are no longer equal and the resulting sum of horizontal forces
has to be taken into account. This results in one horizontal reaction decreasing and
the other increasing in accordance with the size of the added horizontal forces.

2.3.2
Two hinged
As described in chapter 2.2 the static determinancy of a two-hinged arch increases by
one as the crown is removed and the two-hinged arch will therefore be a structure of
п¬Ғrst degree static indeterminacy. At such the structure has a redundant horizontal
reaction which cannot be determined as described for the three-hinged arch [12].
13
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 2
Section 2.3
The diп¬Җerence in static analysis of section forces between the two- and three-hinged
arch is the determination of the redundant reaction. As all other aspects are equal to
that of the statically determinate structure only this aspect of the two-hinged arch
will be elaborated in this chapter. The process can be divided into п¬Ғve steps by use
of the п¬Ӯexibility method [13].
Step 1
The п¬Ғrst step revolves around the theoretical removal of the redundant reaction.
This causes the structure to behave as a statically determinate structure. In the case
of an arched structure the reaction removed will be one of the horizontal reactions
causing the support at either A or B to become a roller support. This is shown in
п¬Ғgure 2.10.

Figure 2.10: Removal of the redundant reaction
Step 2
For the new statically determined structure the moment equations (M 0) are deter-
mined for each section of the arch. Here a section is given as a length interval of
the arch curve where no new forces are added. The division into sections start from
either support A or B.
Step 3
All loads are removed from the structure and in place of the redundant reaction a
unit load of 1 kN is placed. Now for the same sections as in step 2 the moment
equations (M 1) are calculated.

2.5
Optimal arch
To construct the optimal arch in regards to costs and environmental impact the
optimal shape of the arch has to chosen to minimise the occurrence of sectional
forces. Choosing the optimal shape of the arch can be simpliп¬Ғed to the following two
aspects.
вҖў Rise-to-span ratio
вҖў Arch function type
2.5.1
Arch function type
When designing an arch the distribution of forces is very dependent on the type of
arch center line function chosen as the curvature of the arch. Stated in [7] the three
functions types described in 2.2 each have their own optimal load case.
17
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 2
Section 2.5
вҖў Parablic: The parabolic arch function is optimal in the case of uniformly
distributed loads in the horizontal directions. This could include the imposing
wind load, seismic loads or accidental loads.
вҖў Catenary: The catenary arch function is, as opposed to the parabolic arch,
optimal when dealing with vertically distributed loads from the own weight of
the arch.
вҖў Circular: The circular arch function combines the beneп¬Ғts of the two other
function types. Be it not to the same degree and can be considered a hybrid
solution suited for both cases.
A further beneп¬Ғt to the circular arch function is the constant curve. This ensures
that if multiple smaller elements are needed to complete the arch these can all be
produced to the same size. The production process would decrease in both time and
price as assembly would become signiп¬Ғcantly easier.
2.5.2
Rise-to-Span ratio
The eп¬Җects of the rise-to-span ratio are investigated by use of multiple ratios on an
example arch following a circular center line curve with a span of Ls = 30m and
only the dead load of a 1 m2 concrete cross-section acting globally throughout the
entire span. Each rise-to-span ratio is simulated in Autodesk Robot for both a two-
and three-hinged arch and the resulting correlation between horizontal reactions and
the moments are shown in tables 2.4 and 2.5. The table showcases what is to be
expected to be the forces which governs the design arch and corresponding foun-
dation. ItвҖҷs assumed that the need for a larger moment capacity in the arch will
result in additional need for concrete and reinforcement where a larger horizontal
reactions primarily resulting in the need for a larger foundation and corresponding
reinforcement.

corresponding negative bending moment for each of the rise-to-span ratios used.
19
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 2
Section 2.5
Figure 2.12: Two-Hinged (Negative Bending Moment / Horizontal Reaction)
Figure 2.13: Three-Hinged (Negative Bending Moment / Horizontal Reaction)
It becomes apparent that for both the two- and three-hinged arches a decrease in rise
results in a reduction of the bending moment present in the arch. This does however
also increase the horizontal reactions meaning a decrease in rise results in an increase
in thrust. For the two-hinged arch the negative bending moments appear to move
towards a zero value at lower rise-to-span ratios. The negative moment is however
not the only moment present.
Special for the two-hinged arch is the occurrence
of both negative- and positive moments. Illustrated in п¬Ғgure 2.14 are the positive
moments for the two-hinged arch.
20
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 2
Section 2.5
Figure 2.14: Two-Hinged (Positive Bending Moment / Horizontal Reaction)
Here the positive moments appear to increase at the rise-to-span ratios at which the
negative moments move towards the zero-line. This correlation of positive moments
and horizontal thrust shows no linearity and starts to deviate from the tendency of
a decrease in bending moment when a decrease in rise occurs. This phenomenon
is explained in Design and Construction of Shallow Reinforced Concrete Arches for
Bridge Structures [16] as the arch acquiring more beam static behaviour as the rise
decreases. ItвҖҷs explained that the critical threshold for two-hinged arches with a span
of 30 meters to keep their majority of arch static behaviour is between 1-8 and 1-12
which it supported by the acquired data as the tendency from 1/12 to 1/8 shows a
linear tendency.
The correlations of both negative- and positive moments in the two-hinged arch
suggests that the design criteria must take both into account. This translates to
the material cross-section having suп¬ғcient moment capacity in both the negative-
and positive cases. A moment curve illustrating the numerical maximum bending
moments is created and shown in п¬Ғgure 2.15.
21
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 2
Section 2.5
Figure 2.15: Two-Hinged (Numerical Maximum Bending Moment / Horizontal Re-
action)
Where the optimal rise-to-span ratios seems to be at 1/6 as the smallest bending
moment occurs here with the horizontal thrust being quite small compared to the
ratios lower.
Finally when choosing which arch type (two- or three-hinged) is the optimal for the
design the occurring moments and horizontal thrust have to be evaluated against
the other. Illustrated in п¬Ғgure 2.16 are the maximum numerical bending moments
as a function of the corresponding horizontal thrust for diп¬Җerent ratios where the
rise-to-span analysis is expanded to also include 1/2 and 1/14.
22
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 2
Section 2.6
Rise: 1/6
Figure 2.16: Two- & Three-Hinged (Numerical Maximum Bending Moment / Hori-
zontal Reaction)
Resulting from the illustration is the conclusion that at the ratio of 1/6 the moments
and horizontal thrusts are close to similar and choosing either type will result in
a close to similar strain on the material in the arch. At rise-to-span ratios higher
than 1/6 the two-hinged is to be preferred and at ratios lower the three-hinged is
preferred.
The choices determining the optimal rise-to-span ratio will then be based of multiple
parameters such as: the strength of underlying bearing soil, the capacity limit of
material in the arch and the possibility to lift the arch as the arch is either lifted
in one (Two-hinged) or two pieces (Three-hinged). If none of these are taken into
account the purely statically analysed optimal arch is a two-hinged arch at the rise-
to-span ratio of 1/6.
2.6
Modern Arch Implementation
The implementation of arches in modern constructions are usually limited to struc-
tures in which a large span is required or a large constant dead load is applied.
To optimize the design of the arched pearl-chain proposal, with the primary arch
replacing the traditional roof and facade walls of the stable building, a study of
current arched structures in modern construction design is made to gain inspiration
and avoid construction errors. The study will include noticeable arched structures
erected recently where these can be divided into arches made primarily of steel, wood
23
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 2
Section 2.6
or concrete. Each arch type will have itвҖҷs static function and corresponding beneп¬Ғts
stated.
2.6.1
Steel Arches
Steel arches are typically utilized when larger spans are needed and itвҖҷs undesirable
to have columns interrupting the overall aesthetic of the building.
This solution
commonly appears in larger concert halls or airport hangars as the size of airplanes
and transport of equipment in the structure requires a large amount of free space
both vertically and horizontally. Examples of these constellations are shown in п¬Ғgures
2.17 and 2.18.
Figure 2.17: Large steel truss arched structure of Switz landп¬Ғll [17]
Figure 2.17 shows how the arched steel structure implements the principle of a truss
system to further optimize the amount of steel needed to meet the requirements of
the bearing capacity. The truss system uses a simple distribution of forces which
causes each element of the truss system to be either in compression or tension.
24
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 2
Section 2.6
Figure 2.18:
Steel arched structure in
K.B Hallen [18]
Figure 2.19: Steel arched structure by
use of thin-plated steel beams [19]
Figure 2.18 shows the construction of the new K.B Hall in Denmark. The arched
structure is a hybrid arch system which makes use of two primary stabilizing truss
frames and the rest being solid beams bent to п¬Ғt the curve of the arch. Here a
smaller span makes it possible to have a simpler arched construction than the previ-
ously stated steel arch.
The last typical steel arch structure is shown in п¬Ғgure 2.19 where the arched structure
is a simple thin plated continuous arch nailed together. This causes the structure to
ensure the stability against horizontal forces by instating a disc eп¬Җect.
Common for both of the larger arched steel structures is the horizontal connection
method between the arches. Here minor horizontal steel beams connects each arch
to the next- and previous arch which, when a stiп¬Җelement capable of functioning as
a stiп¬Җdisc is installed as rooп¬Ғng, allows all arches to function as one when imposing
horizontal loads, such as wind and seismic loads, are acting on the structure. The
overall stability of the structure is then ensures by diagonal steel rods which when
in tension transfers the loads to the foundation.
When summarizing steel arches the deciding factors for choosing whether a steel
truss system is required is the length of the span and the corresponding sections
forces. Here a truss system can handle signiп¬Ғcantly more load while maintaining a
relatively low material amount compared to the required steel amount needed for a
single steel arch. As this applies for all sizes of arched structures the steel elements
used for the arch can, in small scale building, be thin-plated steel beams as shown
in п¬Ғgure 2.19. Here no transverse connection beams are needed as the thin-plated
steel beams are simply fastened together. This solution needs no additional system
to handle the horizontal loads as the disc function of the structure is ensured by the
arched themselves and the transport of the forces automatically occurs in the arches.
25
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 2
Section 2.6
By using steel as the selected material for arched structures it allows for more strain
to be put into the material and a higher rise-to-span ratio can be used. This is due
to the correlation described in chapter 2.2 where an increase in the rise of the arch
increases the strain on the arch but reduces the horizontal thrust at the supports.
2.6.2
Wooden Arches
Arched structures by use of wood as the primary building material are, like the steel
arches, used for longer spans where itвҖҷs undesirable to have columns interrupting the
span. The wood does however have a lower strength than steel and a larger amount
of wood is therefore required for the arches to withstand the imposing load. To bet-
ter utilize the wood a typical solution is wooden truss frames which is however only
used for straight rooп¬Ғng elements (such as the reference stable in wood).
Larger wooden arches are constructed as three-hinged arches as the size of each
element is too large to transport as one arch due the amount of material required
to ensure the safety of the structure. An example of this is seen below where the
construction process of the CPH Racehall is illustrated.
Figure 2.20: Wooden three-hinged arches
in Racehall CPH [20]
Figure 2.21:
Concrete foundation of
arches in Racehall CPH [20]
The stability of the go kart race hall is ensured as with the steel arches. Noticeable in
this solution is the design of the foundations where the concrete follows the curve of
the arch. This ensures a smooth transition of internal forces from the support to the
load bearing soil and minimizes the occurrence of critical local stress. Furthermore
itвҖҷs seen that the foundation is noticeably longer than it is wide. This is due to the
horizontal thrust which moves the eп¬Җective area of the underlying load bearing soil
26
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 2
Section 2.6
further away from connection point [21]. This solution concept is a optimal solution
for the design of arches with a low rise-to-span ratio.
2.6.3
Concrete Arches
Concrete arches are not commonly used in occupied structures due to the larger costs
associated with the construction. Commonly is however the use of concrete arches
in bridges which includes both larger and smaller bridges.
Figure 2.22 shows the rocky creek bridge constructed in 1932 with itвҖҷs primary static
system revolving around the bottom concrete arch which is ensured to be in constant
compression due to the large amount of constant vertical dead load being applied.
The issue of the horizontal thrust is then solved by the arch springings being placed
directly into the rocky mountain side and transferring the enormous horizontal thrust
to the rocks.
Figure 2.22: Rocky Creek concrete bridge [22]
The rocky creek bridge showcases the use of concrete arches in the largest possible
scale.
Figure 2.23 shows the smallest possible scale.
Here the utilization of the
concrete arch is limited by a smaller span and a rise-to-span ratio of 1/2 is used.
According to the analysis performed in chapter 2.5 this causes the horizontal thrust
to be minimized and the bending moment to increase.

Figure 2.23: Small precast concrete arch [23]
As the bending moment increases drastically in this case the concrete arch is required
to be signiп¬Ғcantly reinforced and to optimize the static function of the arch itвҖҷs
constructed as a three-hinged arch as shown in п¬Ғgure 2.24.
Figure 2.24: Small precast concrete arch construction illustration [23]
As shown in chapter 2.5 the three-hinged arch only experiences negative moments,
meaning the arch will by nature try to expand outwards which is counteracted by the
surrounding soil, as shown in п¬Ғgure 2.25, making this implementation of the higher
ratio possible and optimal.

Chapter 3
Pearl Chain Reinforcement Arches
3.1
Theory
The principle of pearl-chain technology, as described in Design of Sustainable Light
Concrete Structures [7], is based on the idea of multiple pearls being strung together
by a centrally placed string. The principle was originally thought of by professor
Kristian Dahl Hertz in 2008 as he based it on a small toy animal in his possession
which had a small string throughout and stood up when the string was tensioned.
The principle has since evolved to larger concrete elements being tensioned together
by steel cables in an arched shape as illustrated in п¬Ғgure 3.1.
Mortar Joint
Concrete Element
Cable Duct
Figure 3.1: Pearl-chain example arch
Between each concrete element a thin layer of mortar is cast which ensures suп¬ғcient
load transfer capabilities between each arch elements. As the principle utilizes the
natural advantage provided by an arched structure to remain largely in compression
and to reduce the imposing section forces the amount of concrete otherwise needed
30
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 3
Section 3.2
for similar spans is signiп¬Ғcantly reduced.
The principle has quickly proven a valid solution to the increased costs associated
with typical arched concrete structures as no special scaп¬Җolding or moulding is needed
for the construction process. The pearl-chain principle allows for each element to
remain identical if the arch is designed using the circular center line. The production
process and material costs can therefore be signiп¬Ғcantly reduced as the same mould
will be used multiple times and the time of construction for each element would
decrease compared to the otherwise needed scaп¬Җolding. The potential error in casting
is also minimized as each element could be checked for visible cracks and other error
before being tensioned into the п¬Ғnal arched shape.
3.2
Vorgod Creek Bridge
The principle of pearl-chain technology has multiple constructed implementations
which mimics the implementation in an arch format. One particularly noticeable
implementation is the Vorgod Creek Bridge constructed in Jutland - Denmark. The
technology was implemented as a bridge solution for an old bridge which was to be
rebuild. As seen in п¬Ғgure 3.3 the bridge has three spans with the main span being a
pearl-chain arch and the two outer spans half pearl-chain arches.
Figure 3.2: Vorgod Creek bridge [24]
The structural arches were constructed from super-light concrete deck [7] stringed
together as seen in п¬Ғgure 3.3.
31
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 3
Section 3.3
Figure 3.3: Pearl-chain bridge [7]
The problem surrounding the thrust at the main arch springings was solved by the
addition of a centrally placed concrete piece which withstood the thrust. The total
thrust at the main-arch springings can then be estimated by the destabilizing thrust
force from the main-arch and the stabilising thrust force from the half-arch. This
correlation can be simpliп¬Ғed as shown in equation 3.1 and results in the destabilizing
force which the underlying foundation is veriп¬Ғed for.
RHm вҲ’RHh = RDH
(3.1)
Where RHm is the thrust from the main-arch, RHh the thrust from the half-arch and
RDH the destabilizing thrust. The concrete piece transferring the trust is then in
compression resulting from the total thrust force as shown in equation 3.2.
RHh + RHh = RH
(3.2)
Where RH is the total horizontal force experience by the support center piece.
3.3
Intended Element Angle Design
As each concrete element has to be cheaply mass produced a simple casting bed is be
used for production at the factory. This means that the elements themselves canвҖҷt
follow the curve of the arch directix. Each element must therefore be cast with an
intended angle at each element end.
The intended angle is precisely calculated to ensure the curvature to follow the arch
function. At the start of the design process for pearl-chain arched structures the span
and rise are the п¬Ғrst parameters to decide on. These must follow one of the arch
32
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 3
Section 3.3
functions stated in chapter 2.2 and resulting in the angle change which each element
must follow. As stated in that same chapter the optimal function is the circular
function and at such the arch is programmed to п¬Ғt the required rise and span. The
intended angle must ensure that the cable duct meant for the post-tensioned steel
strands is placed centrally at each element end. An example of this is shown in п¬Ғgure
3.4 where the placement of the cable duct is shown to have the smallest vertical cover
distance at the center of each element and that the cable duct is central at the each
element end.
ОІ
Min
Max
Figure 3.4: Placement of post-tensioned cable duct in the crown concrete element
The process of cable duct distance repeats itself for each element meaning that the
two worst cases of bearing capacity in each element will occur at the end and at the
middle.
The process of calculating the intended angle starts with the theoretical placement
of the crown element. The concrete crown element is measured to п¬Ғt the cable arch
function center line resulting in the center line being in the middle of the element
end. If no mortar was added between each concrete element, the angle ОІ resulting
from this п¬Ғtting would be suп¬ғcient to ensure a identical position of the center line
for each concrete element. To incorporate the mortar layer in the design of element
angle the triangle shown in п¬Ғgure 3.5 is used to estimate the additional angle change
cause by the mortar layer.

3.4
Post tensioning
For the individual concrete elements in the pearl-chain arch to stay together the
elements are post-tensioned at the site. By use of a centrally placed cable duct steel
strands are banded together and tensioned. The cable duct is designed to follow
the curve of the arch function and ensures a constant compression force in all of the
concrete elements. Before each tensioned steel strand is released the duct is grouted
with mortar to ensure the transfer of tension force to the concrete, keeping the cross-
section in compression.
34
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 3
Section 3.6
Around the end of each cable duct is the need for additional reinforcement as the
area here is usually not suп¬ғcient to transfer the loads during the tensioning process.
Here spiraled conп¬Ғnement reinforcement is the typical solution
3.5
Mortar joints
Between each concrete element in the pearl-chain arch is a connection of mortar. The
mortar in each connection is required to provide suп¬ғcient bearing capacity to transfer
forces between each concrete section when hardened. The mortar must also minimize
shrinkage as any lack of connection between the sections caused by the shrinkage
would further strain the remainder of the mortar. Lastly the mortar must be quick
to harden as the pearl-chain arch is expected to be assembled at the constructions
site and lifted into place shortly after the mortar is applied. In Homogeneity and
strength of mortar joints in pearl-chain bridges [25] mortars fulп¬Ғlling all requirements
are examined and as a result a mortar with suп¬ғcient strength parameters relative to
hardening time is found. The mortar is classiп¬Ғed as a commercialized high-strength
self-compacting mortar. No material composition for the mortar was found and at
such will not be stated in this thesis.
3.6
Ties
As stated in chapter 2.3.1 the use of an arched structure introduces horizontal out-
going reactions at both supports. To withstand these and ensure a safe structure
a large foundation in concrete or a direct connection to a solid rock mountain side
could be used. If the conditions do not support these solutions its possible to install
a horizontal tie which functions in tension to withstand the horizontal thrust. This
tie can either be a regular steel strand or a post-tensioned steel strand depending on
the needed force. The principle is shown in п¬Ғgure 3.7.
Steel Tie
Figure 3.7: Placement of horizontal steel tie from each arch support
35
==PAGE:50==
Chapter 3
Section 3.6
By choosing this method of additional reinforcement in the foundation a lot of ma-
terial can be saved as the strain on the foundation decreases greatly overall as does
the need for vertical load to avoid sliding. This is further elaborated in chapter 5.5.
36

Wind Load
To simulate the wind load acting on arched roof structure the method described in
Eurocode 1991 [26], which describes a procedure for load determination on cubular
structures, is used and the attained values are added to the FEM program Autodesk
Robot which combines all load types.
As described in EN 1991 the mean wind velocity imposing on a structure can be
calculated using equation 4.5.
wm(z) = cr(z) В· c0(z) В· wb
(4.5)
Where z is the height of the structure in meters, cr(z) the roughness factor, c0(z)
the orthography factor (set to 1.0) and wb the basis wind velocity.
The basis wind velocity can be determined using the equation 4.6.
wb = cdir В· cseason В· wb0
(4.6)
39
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 4
Section 4.1
Where cseason is the seasonal factor (set to 1.0 in this project), wb0 the initial basis
wind velocity dependant on the structures placement in Denmark (set to 24 m/s
in this project) and cdir the directional factor which introduces a reduction factor
for wind directions other than west. The stable structures to be calculated are all
assumed to experience western wind as the critical wind case and a directional factor
of 1.0 is used.
The roughness factor in equation 4.5 describes the roughness of the terrain in which
the structure is erected and is calculated using equation 4.7.
cr(z) = kr В· ln

Chapter 5
Design of Pearl-chain Arch
Solution
5.1
Reference Stables
The two reference stable buildings, from which the dimensions of the proposed so-
lution are to be п¬Ғtted, are shown in п¬Ғgures 5.1 and 5.2. Figure 5.1 shows the stable
building located at BjerregЛҡardsvej 1, 6893 Hemmet, with itвҖҷs primary structural sys-
tem being made out of wood, and п¬Ғgure 5.2 located at Ny Ulsevej 3, 4690 Haslev,
with itвҖҷs structural system being made out of steel.
Figure 5.1: 3D view of the structural parts in the wood stable building
43
==PAGE:58==
Chapter 5
Section 5.2
Figure 5.2: 3D view of the structural parts in the steel stable building
To notice is the distance between each structural frame where the stable build with
steel frames has a much larger distance between each frame when compared to the
wooden stable. The steel frames are placed every 5 meters and the wooden frames
every 1 meter. The design of the pearl-chain arch is calculated by assuming a distance
to the next arch of 5 meters.
5.2
Design Criteria
Design of the pearl-chain arch is done according to the material and structural criteria
stated in EN 1992 [27] and includes the design of concrete and reinforcement steel.
The design of the pearl-chain arch is furthermore governed by the criteria to inner and
outer dimensions in the building. Here the needed inner free space is the governing
factor.
5.2.1
Dimension criteria
The two stables share inner dimensions and at such the same inner dimensions are
used to design the pearl-chain arch. The dimensions are shown in п¬Ғgure 5.3 for the
wooden stable and in 5.4 for the steel stable. The minimum dimensions stated are; a
minimum inner width of 25.7 meters, a room height of 2.4 meters and a total height
of 6.4 meters.
44
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 5
Section 5.2
Figure 5.3: Height and width criteria for PC arch in the wooden stable
Figure 5.4: Height and width criteria for PC arch in the steel stable
When modifying the design of the arch the optimal rise-to-span ratio described in
chapter 2.2 is taken into account.
5.2.2
Material Requirements
Concrete
The type/strength of concrete chosen for the arch is governed by factors of such as
needed compression capacity, minimum density as a function of the environmental
class and the strength relation between mortar and concrete, see chapter 5.7.4. In
accordance with the selected environmental exposure class the concrete type is cho-
sen in EN 1992 table 4.3N [27] as C45/55 for the arch itself and C30/37 for the
foundation.
Tensioned Steel Reinforcement
The requirements to tensioned steel components are stated c.f EN 1992 3.3 [27]. Fig-
ure 5.5 presents the assumption of tension strength possible for each steel component.

steel
In the analysis to come the tension strength is assumed to follow the design curve
until yielding and and upon yielding it will follow the lower limit estimated as the
yield strength. In accordance with EN 1992 3.3.1.5 [27] the design of initial tension
force is to be based on the limits of the 0.1 % proof stress fp0.1k, the relaxation of the
characteristic proof strength fpk and the ultimate strain limit Пөuk. By usage of EN
1992 5.10.3.1 the limit to initial tension force for post-tensioned steel components
is given as 5.1.
Pinitial = min (0.8 В· fpk; 0.9 В· fp0.1k) В· As
(5.1)
Where As is the cross section area of the steel component. For pre-stressed compo-
nents the loss of tension force is given c.f EN 1992 3.3.2.(5), which introduces the
expected tension force as. 5.2.
Ploss = 0.70 В· fpk
(5.2)
No further tension loss is to be expected for pre-tensioned components c.f [28]. The
post-tensioned components have multiple tension losses which must be taken into
account, see chapter 5.5.1.

5.3
Suggested Design
To meet the design criteria of minimum usable volume the pearl-chain has a span of
34 meters and a rise of 5.667 meters to the center of the crown resulting in a rise-to-
span ratio of 1/6. The span in this case is governed by the optimal ratio of 1/6 as
stated in 2.2 and at such is the lowest possible span achievable with the minimum
needed free room height and width as the limiting factor. The п¬Ғnal proposed design
is shown in п¬Ғgure 5.6.
47

Figure 5.6: Illustration of the п¬Ғnal arch design
The arch is constructed from 7 identical concrete C45/55 elements as well as two
identical concrete C45/55 end elements, which have a slightly larger cross section to
ensure the transfer of load from the arch to the concrete foundation. As illustrated
in п¬Ғgure 5.7 the cross-section of the end elements increases from the cross-section of
the regular elements.
Figure 5.7: Illustration of pearl-chain arch end elements
Each regular concrete element measures 550mm in height and 200mm in depth
whereas the end elements reaches a width of 500mm and a height of 780 to п¬Ғt the
curve induced at the foundation. At element end suп¬ғcient space is ensured for the
post-tensioning to occur and additionally, to ensure a safe transfer of the forces from
the arch to the foundation, two transfer sides with a width of 100mm are designed.
Each element end is constructed with an angle of 94.03o calculated as stated in chap-
ter 3.3 to ensure each element follows the arch center line. The elements are cast
with two pre-tensioned strands at the top and at the bottom as shown in п¬Ғgure 5.8.
This is needed to provide suп¬ғcient bearing capacity during lifting of single elements.
48
==PAGE:63==
Chapter 5
Section 5.3
Figure 5.8: Illustration of the placement of reinforcement in each concrete element
Curved throughout each element is a cable duct of Гҳ40 which is meant to hold 4
Гҳ15 post-tensioned strands. The curve of the duct ensures that the location of the
duct relative to each concrete element remains the same with the minimum cover
layer being in the middle of each element and the duct being in the center of the
element at element end. Between each element is a 20mm mortar layer chosen as
mortar type A [25]. The proposed foundation detail is as shown in п¬Ғgure 5.9
Figure 5.9: Detail of the proposed foundation for each pearl-chain arch
The foundation is designed with a concrete of strength C30/37 and reinforcement in
the two main structural components. The foundation shaft is designed with 7 Гҳ10
stirrups c.c 200mm for shear capacity and 12 Гё16 vertical reinforcement to ensure
the shaft has suп¬ғcient moment capacity when subjected to the horizontal load from
the arch. The foundation bottom plate is also designed with 4 Гҳ16 reinforcement
49
==PAGE:64==
Chapter 5
Section 5.4
rods in the x direction and and with 13 Гҳ16 reinforcement rods in the y direction.
Lastly to ensure no sliding occurs a post-tensioned 3 Гҳ12 strand is installed. The
strand is embedded into a concrete deck (which is present in the reference stables)
and can utilize the compression strength of that concrete if sliding occurs inwards.
As each arch is designed with a distance of 5 meters to the next the stable building
can extend to any required length if only the length is a multiple of 5 meters. The
building with a length of 80 meters (the length of the other stables) is shown in
п¬Ғgure 5.10 to compare the overall look of the static system to the ones shown for the
wooden- and steel type stable building in п¬Ғgures 5.1 and 5.2.
Figure 5.10: Isometric view of potential arch placement
The stability system of the entire structure is assured as with the reference stables
and will not be elaborated.

5.5
ULS - Bearing Capacity Veriп¬Ғcation
Veriп¬Ғcation of the ultimate limit state bearing capacity of the pearl-chain arch and
correlating foundation is based on the methods stated in EN 1992, chapter 6 [27].
When veriп¬Ғed in the ultimate limit state all structural components are checked in
terms of their resistance against all imposing load combinations where the resistance
towards moment, axial force and shear is veriп¬Ғed.
5.5.1
PCA - Tension Losses
This chapter presents the calculation method for losses in tension force as well as a
showcase of the п¬Ғnal total tension loss for the post-tensioned strands in the pearl-
chain arch for which the arch is to be veriп¬Ғed.
Friction Loss
The immediate loss of tension force throughout the post-tensioned concrete elements
can be divided into two categories; intentional and unintentional. The intentional
losses comes from the chosen curvature of the cable duct. The tensioning of the steel
cable causes the cable to interact with the cable duct sides and results in a loss of ten-
sioning force. To simplify the procedure of estimating the intentional losses a friction
52
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 5
Section 5.5
loss coeп¬ғcient is introduced as Вө. The unintentional loss comes from the wobbling
of the cable throughout the cable duct. This is estimated by a wobble coeп¬ғcient Оә
which estimates the loss as a function of the change in angle from the entrance angle.
By combining the intentional and unintentional losses the loss in tension force can
be described by equation 5.6.
Pfric(x) = Pjack В· (1 вҲ’eвҲ’Вө+ОәВ·x)
(5.6)
A visual assessment of the the loss throughout the concrete element shows the loss
to function linearly for both one- and two- sided jacking as shown in п¬Ғgure 5.15.

without shear reinforcement can be calculated as shown in 5.19 [29].

capacity of concrete calculated in equation 5.21.

(5.20)

the angle between the horizontal x-axis and the cable duct at the point in which the
prestressing force contributes.

5.6
SLS - Serviceability Limit State Veriп¬Ғcation
EN 1992, chapter 7 [27] introduces the needed veriп¬Ғcation cases for the serviceability
limit state as:
вҖў Limitation of stress in the concrete sections
вҖў Control of cracks
вҖў Control of deп¬Ӯection
63
==PAGE:78==
Chapter 5
Section 5.6
Where if the stress in the cross-section is limited to pure compression in all load cases
the control of cracks and deп¬Ӯection can be neglected. The compressive strength of
the concrete is limited according to DS/EN 1992 - 7.2 [27] and states a reduction of
the strength by a factor Оіc = 1.2.
The process of veriп¬Ғcation begins with the calculation of internal stress distribution
for each load type applied. The distribution is assumed linear by use of navies stress
distribution shown in 5.29.
Пғ(z) = N
A + M
Iy
В· z
(5.29)
Where z is the height of the cross-section from the geometrical center line. Each
applied load type is analysed in Autodesk Robot and the corresponding axial force
N and moment M applied according to equation 5.29. For the load case of the pre-
and post tensioned strands each internal moment (3) contributes to the total stress
and is applied accordingly. The load cases to be checked are the following:
вҖў SLS-1: Deadload + Tension Forces
вҖў SLS-2: Lifting process + Tension Forces

5.7
Construction
5.7.1
Element Assembly
The process of assembling each pearl-chain arch requires multiple steps where the
structural integrity of each element is ensured at the end of each step. This translates
to a check of material state where the concrete should not be damaged. The process
is primarily based on the steps described in Assembly and lifting of pearl-chain arches
[31] and Construction and Design of Post-tensioned Pearl-chain Bridges using SL-
Technology [24].
Element transport
As each pearl-chain consists of 7 identical elements with two identical abutment ele-
ments which have a slightly larger cross section they are all transported as singular
elements. Each element is to be lifted from the transportation vehicle and placed ac-
cording to the latter assembly position. The elements are lifted using two anchorage
points as shown in п¬Ғgure 5.27
66
==PAGE:81==
Chapter 5
Section 5.7
evgen926@m ail.ru
Figure 5.27: Illustration of lifting with anchorage points and needed lifting height for
a single concrete element by use of a truck-crane
Illustrated in the п¬Ғgure is the use of a truck-crane which can both transport and
lift the singular elements. This solution for transportation is proposed but the п¬Ғnal
solution will be up to the entrepreneur hired for the construction of the stable.
Mortar joint
As all concrete elements of the arch have been placed on the ground according to the
design shown in chapter 5.3 a 20mm space between each element is ensured. Each
20mm space is then encased on three of the four side, leaving the top open. A small
cap is placed to protect the cable duct. The previously stated mortar mix is then
poured into the opening and left to harden. The process of hardening should be a
minimum of 2 days [31].
Post-tensioning
The procedure of post-tensioning the pearl-chain arch is performed by two-sided
jacking.
Each jack pulls with the design force corresponding to the total initial
tension force stated in chapter 5.5. As the cable duct is already placed according
to the curve in each element the method of post-tensioning can be simpliп¬Ғed to the
steps stated below.
вҖў 1. Pull the tendons through the cable duct
вҖў 2. Gather the steel tendons in the anchor head
вҖў 3. The tendons are jacked with the desired force
вҖў 4. The tendons are cut to end at the anchor head
67
==PAGE:82==
Chapter 5
Section 5.7
вҖў 5. A permanent anchor grout cap is installed and the cable duct is grouted
using the small opening at the top.
The п¬Ғnal constellation of the anchor is shown in п¬Ғgure 5.28 and based on the method
of post-tensioning described in [32].
Figure 5.28: Post-tension anchor constellation
Where the speciп¬Ғc dimensions of the anchor plate can be seen in п¬Ғgure 5.29
Figure 5.29: Illustration of mono-strand anchor plate
68
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 5
Section 5.7
5.7.2
Arch Lifting Process
Lifting Vehicles
As each pearl-chain arch has been assembled and post-tensioned the next step of
construction is the lifting and installation of the arches. Firstly an arch is lifted us-
ing two anchorage points in the middle of the third element from each side. For each
element a minimum vertical clearance of 1000mm is used to minimize the chances of
bumping into other structural parts and to provide the required space for connection
work between the arch and foundation.
Choosing a lifting vehicle depends on multiple aspects of the chosen construction
side as well as the total building size. By use of BMS cranes two options appear:
Truck-cranes or mobile cranes. The pros for each of the two lifting solutions are
summarised in table 5.8.
Table 5.8: Lifting vehicles - Pros & Cons
Pros
Cons
Truck-crane
Lower rental costs
Combines transport and lifting
Low lifting capacity
Short extension arm
Mobile crane
Long extension arm
High lifting capacity
High rental costs

reduces the construction time.

Each arch is assembled on itвҖҷs side close to itвҖҷs corresponding foundations. This

All arches should be assembled before the crane arrives which would minimize the
renting period of the vehicle.

5.7.4
Foundation Connection
Each pearl-chain arch is connected to the foundation using a connection of mortar
between the two, seen in п¬Ғgure 5.9, and is applied in multiple steps. Initially multiple
plastic wedges are placed as shown in п¬Ғgure 5.36 with each wedge having the thickness
required for the mortar connection (20mm) and are used to ensure the designed span
is met.
Figure 5.36: Connection between pearl-chain arch and foundation by required plastic
wedges (cyan)
73
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 5
Section 5.7
As the end element of the arch is placed on the foundation the room between is п¬Ғlled
with the chosen mortar stated in [25]. To ensure the shape of the mortar remains
intact throughout the hardening process of the mortar the solution is encased by
wooden panels from all sides bar the top. The process of mortar pouring is shown in
п¬Ғgure 5.37 and is continued until the mortar reaches the top of the created casting
form.
Figure 5.37: Mortar pouring in the space between pearl-chain and foundation with
an encasement of wooden panels
The mortar used for the connection between arch and foundation cannot be the same
as the mortar between each arch element. This is due to the designed function of the
foundation to allow minor rotation and function as a simple support. At such the
mortar is required to be weaker than the concrete in the pearl-chain arch. Proposed
by Phillip S. Halding, in a yet to be published paper, is a mortar of lower strength
which is veriп¬Ғed to function as a simple support. As the experiment results are yet
to be oп¬ғcially published the composition of the mortar is not stated.
Two days after the initial casting process the frames can be removed as the mortar has
hardened suп¬ғciently [31]. The plastic wedges are not removed and remain encases
in the mortar. The п¬Ғnal connection is illustrated in п¬Ғgure 5.38.

Chapter 6
LCA - Life Cycle Analysis
The following chapter estimates the environmental impact of the structural compo-
nents in the designed pearl-chain arches and compares that to the impact of the
structural components in the steel and wooden stable building of similar dimensions.
The environmental impact is estimated by use of an life cycle analysis (LCA). The
analysis is divided into multiple sections/steps as shown in п¬Ғgure 6.1 where the steps
included are those relevant for a pure environmental impact estimation of the material
themselves and not the stages involving the construction and use/maintenance of the
structure [33]. The LCA life cycle categories initially excludes are: A4-5, B1-7 and
C1-2.
Figure 6.1: LCA analysis stages [34]
The steps included in the pure material analysis are:
вҖў A1: Material Supply
вҖў A2: Material Transport
76
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 6
Section 6.0
вҖў A3: Material Manufacturing
вҖў C3: Waste Processing
вҖў C4: Disposal
вҖў D: Recycling
The steps C4 and D both relate to an вҖқend of lifeвҖқ case for the materials and are
not considered valid in the same calculation as the material at end of life is either
disposed of or recycled where both cannot occur for the same material. The environ-
mental impact of stage D for each material includes values based on assumptions of
precise reuse of the material in the future with current methods and energy genera-
tion. As this stage includes many potential variables, and the environmental impact
cannot be guarantied, stage D is excluded from the life cycle analysis and stage C4
is used instead as stage C4 provides a environmental impact of the material which
can be guarantied in the future.
For each stable type the volumetric amount is calculated for the structural compo-
nents and based on material data provided by EPD-Denmark [35], ВЁOkobaudat [36]
and [7] a global warming potential (GWP) is estimated. Table 6.1, 6.2 and 6.3 shows
the volume of each construction component for each of the stable types. For complete
calculations see appendix G. Figure 6.2 showcases the placement of the components
stated in table 6.1 with each component being referred to by a number PCA .

From the GWP values stated in table 6.4 itвҖҷs apparent that the values stated for
construction wood diп¬Җers a lot from the other materials. This is due to a generally
occurring trend in which the environmental impact of wood acquisition is calculated
negatively due to the CO2 stored in the wood being taken as a positive contribution
to the overall impact. Whether this approach is correct is widely discussed [7] as this
will favour the wooden constructions in most cases when any material comparisons
are made.
For appliance in the analysis, and to remain impartial when choosing the values for
each material, the mean of the sum for each material type is used if multiple sources
for the same material exist.

The overall global warming potential for the steel stable is signiп¬Ғcantly higher than
for the pearl-chain arch and the wooden stable. This is to be expected as the produc-
tion of steel emits signiп¬Ғcantly more equivalent CO2 than the other materials. The
proposed pearl-chain concrete arch solution shows a decrease of 55% in overall GWP
when compared to the steel stable solution but still concludes a 127% higher GWP
when compared to the wooden stable. As previously stated the trend to include the
CO2 stored in the wood at time of material acquisition is very favorable to wooden
structures and as such structures of other materials cannot be expected to compare
in regards to the global warming potential.

Chapter 8
Span Optimization
The proposed design of the pearl-chain arch is created with a comparison between
stable types in mind. Due to this aspect the natural limitations of an identical usable
volume between all stables types had to be applied. This is due to the additional
economical and environmental aspects associated with a diп¬Җerence in dimensions.
This chapter moves away from the direct stable type comparison while ignoring the
potential additional aspects. Instead only the diп¬Җerence in needed arch material is
investigated and the correlation a increase or decrease in span would cause if the
previously stated optimal rise-to-span ratio is kept at 1/6. This is meant as a start
to a larger analytical analysis which evaluates whether the current stable size is the
optimal. For a complete analysis multiple aspects other than the material use should
be included, see chapter 10.
The course of action when designing the arches with increased and decreased spans
will remain identical to the ones previously presented, shown in appendix D, and
at such the theory will not be presented once again in this chapter. The analysis
is performed by use of four data sets; The proposed arch solution at a span of 34
meters, two decreased spans in 4 meter decrease increments and one increased span
also in a 4 meter increment. Optimally two increased spans at the same increase
increments should be calculated.
During the calculation process it was however
discovered that any span above 39 meters would require a modiп¬Ғed lifting process,
with either more anchorage points or the arch being split into two parts transforming
the static function of the arch into a three-hinged arch. Therefore spans at the length
of 39 meters would not п¬Ғt into the direct static comparison and were not used in the
analysis. The spans and calculated rises are shown in п¬Ғgure 8.1.
85
==PAGE:100==
Chapter 8
Section 8.0
26000
30000
34000
38000
4333
5000
5667
6333
Figure 8.1: Arches to be analysed
Figure 8.2 shows the correlation between the span of the arches and the required
concrete cross-sections area in mm2.
Figure 8.2: Concrete Cross-Section area by change in span
In the calculations the process of cross-section optimisation is based on the needed
capacity type. This translates to the height being increased if the failure occurs due
to insuп¬ғcient bending moment capacity and the width being increased if the failure
occurs due to insuп¬ғcient axial capacity.
The correlation in п¬Ғgure 8.2 shows a relatively linear connection between span in-
crease and needed concrete area. There is however a visible increase in needed area
when approaching larger spans. This is due to the nature of the bending moments
increasing drastically during lifting as previously stated.
Furthermore the need for post-tensioned strands were found to increase by one for
each span increment in order to fulп¬Ғll the serviceability limit state. The increase
correlation is shown in п¬Ғgure 8.3
86
==PAGE:101==

Section 8.0
26m
30m
34m
38m
Figure 8.3: Required post-tensioned steel strands (blue) and grouted cable duct (grey)
The solution of 3 or 4 steel strand is a typical solution as these п¬Ғll the circular cable
duct to a rather large degree. The solution of 2 or 5 strands is an odd solution as
these do not п¬Ғll the cable duct optimally. This aspect can be further optimised and
evaluated in future projects.
The correlation in both the required amount of concrete area and steel strands ap-
pears approximately linear. To keep in mind is however the jump is added post-
tensioning. As the addition of a additional wire in the post-tensioned strand the
eп¬Җects of the non-linear section forces are somewhat counteracted. Furthermore to
consider is how the additional concrete height is added. As seen in п¬Ғgure 8.4 the
volume of concrete added on top adds more than the corresponding parts at the bot-
tom. This is explained by the angle where the further from the bottom a concrete
addition comes the larger the volume is.

To summarize the arches constructed at smaller spans are per meter span both more

88
==PAGE:103==
Chapter 9
Conclusion
The principle of pearl-chain reinforcement technology was theoretically applied in
standard size stable buildings and both the economical and environmental aspects
were compared to that of similar sized currently constructed stable with either a
steel or wooden frame. In both cases the pearl-chain arch solution proved to be
competitive with the total economical costs being lower than the other stables and
the environmental impact being between that of the steel and wooden frame stables.
Initially all relevant theory regarding the historical and static aspects of arches was
presented. This included the methods for determination of reactions and section
forces in the case of either a two- / three-hinged arch and the diп¬Җerent parameters
determining the optimal arch shape. Here it was concluded that the use of a circular
arch directix would allow for similar angles changes and therefor the possibility of
identical elements.
A examination of diп¬Җerent rise-to-span ratios showed a direct correlation between
the ratio and the imposing forces. For the three-hinged arch the bending moment
would decrease as the horizontal thrust increased as a function of a decreasing rise.
The two-hinged arch showed similar behaviour for the negative bending moments
and thrust but the occurring positive bending moments would increase opposite to
the correlation. This lead to an examination of the optimal rise-to-span ratio which
concluded that the ratio of 1-6 was the most optimal for both the two- and three-
hinged arch. To further optimize the arch a small study stating relevant modern
arches and their utilization was made to ensure an optimal design.
The pearl-chain reinforcement theory was presented which included the general prin-
ciple of element connection, edge angle calculation, post-tensioning, joints and ties.
The theory concluded with a showcase of a previous implementation of the technol-
ogy in which super-light concrete decks had been assembled to form and arch bridge.
89
==PAGE:104==
Chapter 9
Section 9.0
The implementation gave an approach to the construction of arches using pearl-chain
technology.
Following the imposing loads stated in eurocode 1991-1 for cubular structures the
critical loads and their calculations methods were stated. All possible load combina-
tions were stated and used in the п¬Ғnal veriп¬Ғcation calculation.
A design of the pearl-chain arch was proposed. The arch had a span of 34 meters,
a rise of 5.67 meters, 7 identical concrete elements with two identical abutment ele-
ments. The arch was tensioning with two C12 pre-tesioned strands in both top and
bottom as well as four C15 post-tensioned strands following the arch directix. The
dimensions of the arch were governed by a minimum volumetric requirement similar
to the other stable types which would ensure that a comparison between only the
structural parts would remain valid. The arch was veriп¬Ғed to have suп¬ғcient bend-
ing moment-, shear and axial capacity in the ultimate limit state as well as pure
compression in the serviceability state allowing for the assumptions that no cracking
would occur in any load case. Lastly the arch was veriп¬Ғed for the lifting process and
a potential construction plan was presented.
Foundations at each arch springing were designed to withstand and safely transfer
the horizontal thrust to load bearing soil. This included the utilization of a tensioned
tie between the each foundation to avoid sliding.
Comparing the environmental impact of each stable type showcased through the
global warming potential, and presented as total kgCO2eqv. for each stable, shows
the pearl-chain arch to better a more environmentally friendly solution than the
steel stable but not the wooden stable. The impact was scaled to represent struc-
tural frames per 5 meters which for both the arch and steel frame was one and for
the wooden frame п¬Ғve. The arch had a equivalent impact of 2576 kgCO2, the steel
frame 6115 kgCO2 and the wooden frames 1135 kgCO2.
Finally a cost estimation of the retail price for each material component in the
diп¬Җerent stable types showed the pearl-chain arch to be a cheaper solution than both
the steel- and wooden stable. This is largely due to the rapid increase in wood retail
prices but can be considered valid for the foreseeable future. The costs were scaled
to frames per 5 meters and concluded a total cost for the pearl-chain arch of 19495
DKK, the steel frame of 61679 DKK and the wooden frames of 30024 DKK.
90
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 10
Future Work
The pearl-chain arch case investigated in this thesis is an isolated case limited by the
dimensions of similar stables as this allows for a direct comparison between struc-
tural materials used.
A potential future analysis could revolve around the optimization of the stable size
with the economical and environmental aspects of the implemented pearl-chain arch
in mind. It was brieп¬Ӯy investigated in chapter 8 how changes to the span of the arch,
while keeping the rise-to-span ratio constant, would impact the amount of needed
concrete and tensioned steel. This investigations showed a tendency for smaller spans
to require a smaller amount of material when scaled to the span of the arch.
When changing the span of an arch the estimation of overall economical and envi-
ronmental impact can however not be simpliп¬Ғed to the change in needed material
per span meter, even though it plays a large part. Additional parameters inп¬Ӯuencing
the analysis are:
вҖў Heating
вҖў Lighting
вҖў Space optimisation
вҖў Animal Transport Procedures
These parameters, and potentially more, would lay the foundation for a complete
analysis and optimisation of the stables.
91

Vertical reaction:
As the loads, span, rise and arch types are the same in this two-hinged arch as in 
the previous example with a three-hinged the vertical reactions are also the same.
вү”
RA
9.141 kN
вү”
RB
8.609 kN
Redundant reaction:
As the two-hinged arch has a static indeterminacy of 1 an redundant reaction 
appears in the structure.
For cases such as this the procedure described in the corresponding theory 
chapter is used.
Step 1. Removal of redundant reaction
In this step the redundant reaction is located and temporarily removed. 
This changes the support condition at the right support from pinned to 
a roller and the structure is now statically determinate.

4 Tension Losses
The losses of the prestressing strands are not calculated as these only occur 
in a straight line of 4 meters, where the posttenioned cable has a curved 
duct over a span of 34 meters which causes losses due to wobble and the 
angle change.

Total immediate loss function
As each elements is prefabricated it's assumed that the potential losses due to 
deformation of concrete are of a small magnitude relative to the draw-in and 
friction losses. The losses due to concrete deformation will therefore be 
neglegted in further calculations.

ULS & SLS - PCA
S163779
5 Positive bending moment capacity (Middle of elements)
The ultimate negative bending moment capacity is determined using the ultimate 
strain assumptions from EN 1992 figure 6.1
Strain Limit A: Yielding of reinforcement
Strain Limit B: Crushing of concrete
Strain Limit C: Pure Compression limit
Concrete Limit:
вү”
ОөcAB
0.0035
Steel Yield Limit:
вү”
ОөpAB
=
вҖ•вҖ•
fpk
Ep
0.009
Yield Limit:
вү”
fyd
=
вҖ•вҖ•
fpk
Оіs
1475 MPa
Calculation Assumptions:
Assumptions are valid for all bending moment capacity calculations
1.
Steel having yielded will remain at the yield strength and no further strength 
due to further strain will be added. Meaning that on the figure below the 
strength estimation remains between point B and C.
[https://zapscience.com/stress-strain-curve/]
2.
The initial strain of a pre/post-tensioned strand is assumed to only contain the 
strain from the tension force directly to the steel and not the concrete around it 
as the concrete contribution is rather small in comparison.
Non-Commercial Use Only
Page 13 of 67
Chapter D
Section D.0
143
==PAGE:158==
Tobias LГёye Christiansen
ULS & SLS - PCA
S163779
3.
The strain in each steel strand is based on the total strain as shown in the figure 
below. The strain contribution to the initial strain from the tension force is taken 
from similar triangles where the assumpion is crushing of the concrete in the top.
[DTU course 11351 - lecture 5]
4.
Strain results are considered valid if the resulting strain in each pre-/post-tensioned 
strand follows a linear function meaning the lower the placement (relative to which 
axis is analyses) the larger the strain.
Non-Commercial Use Only

S163779
7 Negative bending moment capacity (Middle of elements)
The calculations of bending moment capacity reverses the newof the 
crosssection meaning the compression zone will now occur at the bottom of 
the element
7.1 - Strain limit A + B (Middle element - Yield in top reinforcement (P2) + 
Crushing of top concrete, balanced failure))
Strain states:
P2 Strain:
вү”
Оөp2ABm2
=
-
ОөpAB

S163779
9. Bending moment capacity (Mortar)
For calculation of the M-N correlation in mortar the same procedure as for 
concrete is used.
The cross-section of mortar is furthermore assumed equal to that of the 
concrete element whichs means that the mortar connection shares the 
width and height of the concrete cross-section.

Crack Limitation:
As seen from the stress analysis in the previous section the entire concrete cross-
section will be in compression in every load case and therefore no cracking will 
occur.

Sliding & Vertical Capacity (Min load)
The destabilizing and stabilizing horizontal force switch as the horizontal forces 
from the pearl-chain arch are taken as the minimum value to analyze the 
sliding effect of the post-tensioned steel cable.
